### 1...e6

- **Line:** `e4 e6`
- **Side to move:** White
- **FEN:** `rnbqkbnr/pppp1ppp/4p3/8/4P3/8/PPPP1PPP/RNBQKBNR w KQkq - 0 2`
- **Difficulty:** ★★☆☆☆ (2/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material is level. Central pawns: White pawn on e4. Development: White has 0 minor piece(s) out, Black 0. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. It is White's move.

**Why this move.** 1...e6 advances a pawn to e6. The French: solid but a little passive. Black concedes space, plans ...d5 counterplay, and lives with the problem c8-bishop. The exchange line keeps things simple and slightly nicer for White.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** The exchange keeps it simple: symmetric pawns, freer pieces, and none of the closed-centre theory Black hoped to show off. Against e6, the repertoire continues 2.d4.

**What it prevents.** More a constructive move than a preventive one: it denies the opponent easy targets and keeps the position free of weaknesses.

**Candidate alternatives.**
- `e5` _(theoretical)_ — The classical reply: Black stakes an equal claim in the centre. The repertoire answers 2.Nf3. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `d5` _(theoretical)_ — The Scandinavian: Black challenges e4 at once, accepting an early queen excursion after the capture on d5 — time White will collect with developing moves. The repertoire answers 2.exd5. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `c5` _(theoretical)_ — The Sicilian: Black fights for d4 with a wing pawn and aims for unbalanced play. The repertoire answers 2.c3. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `c6` _(theoretical)_ — The Caro-Kann: like the French but with the light-squared bishop's diagonal kept open. The repertoire answers 2.d4. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.

**Tactical checks.** No immediate forcing tactics: no checks, no winning captures and no loose pieces. The position is calm and about plans, not tricks.

**Plans after this move.**
- Finish development quickly — every piece toward the centre.
- Keep or increase the central superiority; meet wing play with central play.
- Choose the right castling: short in quiet lines, long in the Qxd4 setup.

**Memory hook.** Remember 1...e6 as a natural building move in the Scotch Game setup.

**Mistake warning.** Don't drift: keep developing and castle before starting an attack, and don't push pawns in front of your own king without a reason.

### 3.exd5

- **Line:** `e4 e6 d4 d5 exd5`
- **Side to move:** Black
- **FEN:** `rnbqkbnr/ppp2ppp/4p3/3P4/3P4/8/PPP2PPP/RNBQKBNR b KQkq - 0 3`
- **Difficulty:** ★☆☆☆☆ (1/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material favours White by 1 point(s). Central pawns: White pawn on d4, White pawn on d5. Development: White has 0 minor piece(s) out, Black 0. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure: White has doubled pawns on the d-file. Loose/attacked pieces — White: pawn on d5. It is Black's move.

**Why this move.** 3.exd5 the pawn captures the pawn on d5. The clean solution: capture, simplify, and let Black spend time recapturing while White develops.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** Repertoire philosophy: against counter-gambits and symmetry attempts, take what's offered, simplify, and out-develop.

**What it prevents.** Prevents Black from getting the open, active game the gambit promised — the extra tempo goes to White instead.

**Tactical checks.** Checks for Black: Bb4+. Tempting captures for Black: Qxd5, exd5. Loose White pieces: pawn on d5 (undefended).

**Plans after this move.**
- Finish development quickly — every piece toward the centre.
- Keep or increase the central superiority; meet wing play with central play.
- Choose the right castling: short in quiet lines, long in the Qxd4 setup.

**Memory hook.** Take on d5 and develop while they recapture.

**Mistake warning.** Don't drift: keep developing and castle before starting an attack, and don't push pawns in front of your own king without a reason.

### 4.Nf3

- **Line:** `e4 e6 d4 d5 exd5 exd5 Nf3`
- **Side to move:** Black
- **FEN:** `rnbqkbnr/ppp2ppp/8/3p4/3P4/5N2/PPP2PPP/RNBQKB1R b KQkq - 1 4`
- **Difficulty:** ★☆☆☆☆ (1/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material is level. Central pawns: White pawn on d4, Black pawn on d5. Development: White has 1 minor piece(s) out, Black 0. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. It is Black's move.

**Why this move.** 4.Nf3 develops/repositions the knight to f3. Develops with a threat (the e5-pawn). Every developing move that also creates a threat gains time — the opponent must react instead of developing.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** Step two of the plan: develop with a threat, provoke a committal defence of e5, and only then strike with d4.

**What it prevents.** Stops liberating ideas based on ...d5 or holding e5 for free — Black must commit a defender first.

**Tactical checks.** Checks for Black: Bb4+, Qe7+.

**Plans after this move.**
- Play d4 next and open the centre while Black is committed to e5.
- Meet 2...Nf6 (Petroff) with the critical 3.Nxe5.
- Against 2...d6, exchange in the centre and aim for the queen-trade endgame.

**Memory hook.** Develop with a threat — make them defend, not develop.

**Mistake warning.** Don't drift: keep developing and castle before starting an attack, and don't push pawns in front of your own king without a reason.

### 2.c3

- **Line:** `e4 c5 c3`
- **Side to move:** Black
- **FEN:** `rnbqkbnr/pp1ppppp/8/2p5/4P3/2P5/PP1P1PPP/RNBQKBNR b KQkq - 0 2`
- **Difficulty:** ★★☆☆☆ (2/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material is level. Central pawns: White pawn on e4. Development: White has 0 minor piece(s) out, Black 0. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. It is Black's move.

**Why this move.** 2.c3 advances a pawn to c3. The Alapin answer to the Sicilian: prepare d4 so that after ...cxd4 White recaptures with the c-pawn and keeps the full e4+d4 centre — the very thing 1...c5 was meant to prevent.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** The repertoire's anti-Sicilian: skip the theory war, restore the classical centre, and play chess. Black's most principled tries all concede the pawn duo.

**What it prevents.** Prevents the Sicilian's main achievement — a White knight on d4 as a target and an open c-file for Black. Here the c-pawn recaptures instead.

**Tactical checks.** No immediate forcing tactics: no checks, no winning captures and no loose pieces. The position is calm and about plans, not tricks.

**Plans after this move.**
- Play d4 next and recapture with the c-pawn to keep the duo.
- Develop Bd3/Bc4 and Nf3, castle short, and use the space edge.
- Meet ...d5 with exd5 and play against the isolated or symmetric structure.

**Memory hook.** vs the Sicilian: c3 then d4 — rebuild the centre they tried to ban.

**Mistake warning.** Recapturing on d4 with a piece — the c-pawn recapture *is* the point of 2.c3. Also don't skip d4: without it, c3 was just a slow move.

---

### 1...c6

- **FEN:** `rnbqkbnr/pp1ppppp/2p5/8/4P3/8/PPPP1PPP/RNBQKBNR w KQkq - 0 2`

- **Explanation source:** heuristic

Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. It is White's move.

**Why this move.** 1...c6 advances a pawn to c6. The Caro-Kann: like the French but with the light-squared bishop's diagonal kept open. Solid and low-risk — and slow, which lets White grab space.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** Two good options are covered: the space-gaining Advance with the Short setup, and the trick-laden two-knights line with Qe2 ideas. Against c6, the repertoire continues 2.d4.

**What it prevents.** More a constructive move than a preventive one: it denies the opponent easy targets and keeps the position free of weaknesses.

**Candidate alternatives.**
- `e5` _(theoretical)_ — The classical reply: Black stakes an equal claim in the centre. The repertoire answers 2.Nf3. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `d5` _(theoretical)_ — The Scandinavian: Black challenges e4 at once, accepting an early queen excursion after the capture on d5 — time White will collect with developing moves. The repertoire answers 2.exd5. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `e6` _(theoretical)_ — The French: solid but a little passive. The repertoire answers 2.d4. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `c5` _(theoretical)_ — The Sicilian: Black fights for d4 with a wing pawn and aims for unbalanced play. The repertoire answers 2.c3. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.

**Tactical checks.** No immediate forcing tactics: no checks, no winning captures and no loose pieces. The position is calm and about plans, not tricks.

**Plans after this move.**
- Finish development quickly — every piece toward the centre.
- Keep or increase the central superiority; meet wing play with central play.
- Choose the right castling: short in quiet lines, long in the Qxd4 setup.

**Memory hook.** Remember 1...c6 as a natural building move in the Scotch Game setup.

**Mistake warning.** Don't drift: keep developing and castle before starting an attack, and don't push pawns in front of your own king without a reason.

### 2.d4

**Why this move.** 2.d4 advances a pawn to d4. Builds the ideal pawn pair. With pawns on e4 and d4 White controls the four key central squares and both bishops breathe freely.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** Against defences that hand over the centre, the repertoire takes it — two pawns abreast, pieces behind them, and space to attack later.

**What it prevents.** More a constructive move than a preventive one: it denies the opponent easy targets and keeps the position free of weaknesses.

- Castle short and expand on the side where you have more space.

**Memory hook.** Two pawns abreast on e4+d4 — the dream centre, take it when offered.

**Mistake warning.** Don't drift: keep developing and castle before starting an attack, and don't push pawns in front of your own king without a reason.

### 3.e5

- **Line:** `e4 c6 d4 d5 e5`
- **Side to move:** Black
- **FEN:** `rnbqkbnr/pp2pppp/2p5/3pP3/3P4/8/PPP2PPP/RNBQKBNR b KQkq - 0 3`
- **Difficulty:** ★★☆☆☆ (2/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material is level. Central pawns: White pawn on d4, Black pawn on d5, White pawn on e5. Development: White has 0 minor piece(s) out, Black 0. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. It is Black's move.

**Why this move.** 3.e5 advances a pawn to e5. Gains space and time by kicking Black's pieces. The cramping pawn on e5 cuts Black's position in two and fixes a long-term advantage.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** The advance structure: fix the pawn chain, claim the kingside, and manoeuvre behind it (the Short-System treatment against the Caro-Kann).

**What it prevents.** Denies Black's king knight its best square (f6) — a lasting concession you can play against for the whole game.

**Tactical checks.** Checks for Black: Qa5+.

**Plans after this move.**
- Support the spearhead: Nf3, Be2, c3 — the Short System shape.
- Answer ...c5 with Be3/c3, keeping the d4-point rock solid.
- Manoeuvre behind the chain: knights dream of d4, f4 and g5.

**Memory hook.** Push e5: cramp them now, manoeuvre later.

**Mistake warning.** Overextending: pushing pawns beyond what your pieces support hands Black exactly the targets the defence was fishing for.

### 3...dxe4

- **Line:** `e4 c6 Nf3 d5 Nc3 dxe4`
- **Side to move:** White
- **FEN:** `rnbqkbnr/pp2pppp/2p5/8/4p3/2N2N2/PPPP1PPP/R1BQKB1R w KQkq - 0 4`
- **Difficulty:** ★★☆☆☆ (2/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material favours Black by 1 point(s). Central pawns: Black pawn on e4. Development: White has 2 minor piece(s) out, Black 0. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure: Black has doubled pawns on the e-file. Loose/attacked pieces — White: knight on f3; Black: pawn on e4. It is White's move.

**Why this move.** 3...dxe4 the pawn captures the pawn on e4. Releases the central tension and hands White the freer game — the recapture centralises a White piece with gain of time.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** This move follows the repertoire's guiding ideas: open the centre early (d4!), develop with threats, centralise — even the queen, when she cannot be harassed — and steer for structures Black must defend precisely: queen-trade endgames, doubled pawns, kings stuck in the centre. Against dxe4, the repertoire continues 4.Nxe4.

**What it prevents.** It keeps the initiative — the opponent now has to worry about the knight on f3 rather than carrying out their own plan.

**Candidate alternatives.**
- `Bg4` _(positional)_ — Pins the knight to fight indirectly for the central squares — pressure White should question sooner rather than later. The repertoire answers 4.h3. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `d4` _(theoretical)_ — Black pushes past to shut pieces out and grab space — but every advanced pawn needs defenders, and White reroutes to attack it later. The repertoire answers 4.Ne2. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.

**Tactical checks.** Tempting captures for White: Nxe4. Loose White pieces: knight on f3 (attacked by a cheaper piece). Loose Black pieces: pawn on e4 (undefended).

**Plans after this move.**
- Finish development quickly — every piece toward the centre.
- Keep or increase the central superiority; meet wing play with central play.
- Choose the right castling: short in quiet lines, long in the Qxd4 setup.

**Memory hook.** Remember 3...dxe4 as a natural building move in the Scotch Game setup.

**Mistake warning.** Don't drift: keep developing and castle before starting an attack, and don't push pawns in front of your own king without a reason.

### 5.Qe2

- **Line:** `e4 c6 Nf3 d5 Nc3 dxe4 Nxe4 Nf6 Qe2`
- **Side to move:** Black
- **FEN:** `rnbqkb1r/pp2pppp/2p2n2/8/4N3/5N2/PPPPQPPP/R1B1KB1R b KQkq - 2 5`
- **Difficulty:** ★★★★★ (5/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material is level. Neither side has a pawn on the four central squares yet. Development: White has 2 minor piece(s) out, Black 1. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. It is Black's move.

**Why this move.** 5.Qe2 develops/repositions the queen to e2. A modest-looking move loaded with venom: the queen takes the e-file, and careless natural replies run into discovered checks — in some lines even an immediate mate on d6. Learn the *point*, not just the move.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** One of the repertoire's teeth: in both the Petroff and the two-knights Caro-Kann this quiet queen move wins games outright against natural-looking replies (Nc6+ forking king and queen, or even Nd6 mate).

**What it prevents.** Prevents Black from keeping the extra pawn comfortably: the e-file pin means the e4-knight can't just retreat (…Nf6?? loses the queen to the discovered Nc6+).

**Tactical checks.** Checks for Black: Qxd2+.

**Plans after this move.**
- Finish development quickly — every piece toward the centre.
- Keep or increase the central superiority; meet wing play with central play.
- Choose the right castling: short in quiet lines, long in the Qxd4 setup.

**Memory hook.** Qe2: the quiet move that wins queens (Nc6+!) — or mates on d6.

**Mistake warning.** Knowing Qe2 without knowing why: if you can't name the threat (discovered check on the e-file / Nd6 ideas), review the line before playing it — the move only bites with follow-up.

- **Explanation source:** heuristic

Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure: White has doubled pawns on the d-file. It is Black's move.

**Why this move.** 5.exd5 the pawn captures the pawn on d5. The clean solution: capture, simplify, and let Black spend time recapturing while White develops.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** Repertoire philosophy: against counter-gambits and symmetry attempts, take what's offered, simplify, and out-develop.

**What it prevents.** Prevents Black from getting the open, active game the gambit promised — the extra tempo goes to White instead.

**Tactical checks.** No immediate forcing tactics: no checks, no winning captures and no loose pieces. The position is calm and about plans, not tricks.

**Plans after this move.**
- Finish development quickly — every piece toward the centre.
- Keep or increase the central superiority; meet wing play with central play.
- Choose the right castling: short in quiet lines, long in the Qxd4 setup.

**Memory hook.** Take on d5 and develop while they recapture.

**Mistake warning.** Don't drift: keep developing and castle before starting an attack, and don't push pawns in front of your own king without a reason.

### 1...d6

- **Line:** `e4 d6`
- **Side to move:** White
- **FEN:** `rnbqkbnr/ppp1pppp/3p4/8/4P3/8/PPPP1PPP/RNBQKBNR w KQkq - 0 2`
- **Difficulty:** ★★☆☆☆ (2/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material is level. Central pawns: White pawn on e4. Development: White has 0 minor piece(s) out, Black 0. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. It is White's move.

**Why this move.** 1...d6 advances a pawn to d6. A Pirc/Philidor setup: Black concedes the centre for now, intending to chip at it with pieces and pawn breaks later. White simply takes the centre offered.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** White takes the full centre and develops classically — the repertoire refuses to chase shadows on the wings. Against d6, the repertoire continues 2.d4.

**What it prevents.** More a constructive move than a preventive one: it denies the opponent easy targets and keeps the position free of weaknesses.

**Candidate alternatives.**
- `e5` _(theoretical)_ — The classical reply: Black stakes an equal claim in the centre. The repertoire answers 2.Nf3. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `d5` _(theoretical)_ — The Scandinavian: Black challenges e4 at once, accepting an early queen excursion after the capture on d5 — time White will collect with developing moves. The repertoire answers 2.exd5. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `e6` _(theoretical)_ — The French: solid but a little passive. The repertoire answers 2.d4. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `c5` _(theoretical)_ — The Sicilian: Black fights for d4 with a wing pawn and aims for unbalanced play. The repertoire answers 2.c3. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.

**Tactical checks.** Checks for White: Bb5+.

**Plans after this move.**
- Finish development quickly — every piece toward the centre.
- Keep or increase the central superiority; meet wing play with central play.
- Choose the right castling: short in quiet lines, long in the Qxd4 setup.

**Memory hook.** Remember 1...d6 as a natural building move in the Scotch Game setup.

**Mistake warning.** Don't drift: keep developing and castle before starting an attack, and don't push pawns in front of your own king without a reason.

### 2.d4

Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. It is Black's move.

**Why this move.** 2.d4 advances a pawn to d4. Builds the ideal pawn pair. With pawns on e4 and d4 White controls the four key central squares and both bishops breathe freely.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** Against defences that hand over the centre, the repertoire takes it — two pawns abreast, pieces behind them, and space to attack later.

**What it prevents.** More a constructive move than a preventive one: it denies the opponent easy targets and keeps the position free of weaknesses.

**Tactical checks.** No immediate forcing tactics: no checks, no winning captures and no loose pieces. The position is calm and about plans, not tricks.

- Keep the duo intact: c3, Nf3/Nc3 and natural piece play behind the pawns.
- Meet the pawn breaks (...c5, ...d5, ...f6) calmly — support, don't panic-trade.
- Castle short and expand on the side where you have more space.

**Memory hook.** Two pawns abreast on e4+d4 — the dream centre, take it when offered.

**Mistake warning.** Don't drift: keep developing and castle before starting an attack, and don't push pawns in front of your own king without a reason.

### 1...Nf6

- **Line:** `e4 Nf6`
- **Side to move:** White
- **FEN:** `rnbqkb1r/pppppppp/5n2/8/4P3/8/PPPP1PPP/RNBQKBNR w KQkq - 1 2`
- **Difficulty:** ★★☆☆☆ (2/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material is level. Central pawns: White pawn on e4. Development: White has 0 minor piece(s) out, Black 1. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. Loose/attacked pieces — White: pawn on e4. It is White's move.

**Why this move.** 1...Nf6 develops/repositions the knight to f6. Alekhine's Defence: Black invites the pawns forward hoping they overextend. White gains space and time with e5 and d4 — and stays sensible rather than greedy.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** Take the space, decline the provocation: e5 and d4, sensible development, no overextension. Against Nf6, the repertoire continues 2.e5.

**What it prevents.** It keeps the initiative — the opponent now has to worry about the pawn on e4 rather than carrying out their own plan.

- `d5` _(theoretical)_ — The Scandinavian: Black challenges e4 at once, accepting an early queen excursion after the capture on d5 — time White will collect with developing moves. The repertoire answers 2.exd5. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `e6` _(theoretical)_ — The French: solid but a little passive. The repertoire answers 2.d4. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `c5` _(theoretical)_ — The Sicilian: Black fights for d4 with a wing pawn and aims for unbalanced play. The repertoire answers 2.c3. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.

**Tactical checks.** Loose White pieces: pawn on e4 (undefended).

**Plans after this move.**
- Finish development quickly — every piece toward the centre.
- Keep or increase the central superiority; meet wing play with central play.
- Choose the right castling: short in quiet lines, long in the Qxd4 setup.

### 2.e5

- **Line:** `e4 Nf6 e5`
- **Side to move:** Black
- **FEN:** `rnbqkb1r/pppppppp/5n2/4P3/8/8/PPPP1PPP/RNBQKBNR b KQkq - 0 2`
- **Difficulty:** ★★☆☆☆ (2/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material is level. Central pawns: White pawn on e5. Development: White has 0 minor piece(s) out, Black 1. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. Loose/attacked pieces — Black: knight on f6. It is Black's move.

**Why this move.** 2.e5 advances a pawn to e5. Gains space and time by kicking Black's pieces. The cramping pawn on e5 cuts Black's position in two and fixes a long-term advantage.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** The advance structure: fix the pawn chain, claim the kingside, and manoeuvre behind it (the Short-System treatment against the Caro-Kann).

**What it prevents.** Denies Black's king knight its best square (f6) — a lasting concession you can play against for the whole game.

**Candidate alternatives.**
- `Nf3` _(positional)_ — Develops with a threat (the e5-pawn). The repertoire answers 2...e5. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.

**Tactical checks.** Loose Black pieces: knight on f6 (attacked by a cheaper piece).

**Plans after this move.**
- Support the spearhead: Nf3, Be2, c3 — the Short System shape.
- Answer ...c5 with Be3/c3, keeping the d4-point rock solid.
- Manoeuvre behind the chain: knights dream of d4, f4 and g5.

**Memory hook.** Push e5: cramp them now, manoeuvre later.

**Mistake warning.** Overextending: pushing pawns beyond what your pieces support hands Black exactly the targets the defence was fishing for.

---

**Scotch repertoire logic.** This move follows the repertoire's guiding ideas: open the centre early (d4!), develop with threats, centralise — even the queen, when she cannot be harassed — and steer for structures Black must defend precisely: queen-trade endgames, doubled pawns, kings stuck in the centre. Against Nd5, the repertoire continues 3.d4.

**What it prevents.** More a constructive move than a preventive one: it denies the opponent easy targets and keeps the position free of weaknesses.

**Tactical checks.** No immediate forcing tactics: no checks, no winning captures and no loose pieces. The position is calm and about plans, not tricks.

**Plans after this move.**
- Finish development quickly — every piece toward the centre.
- Keep or increase the central superiority; meet wing play with central play.
- Choose the right castling: short in quiet lines, long in the Qxd4 setup.

### 3.d4

- **Line:** `e4 Nf6 e5 Nd5 d4`
- **Side to move:** Black
- **FEN:** `rnbqkb1r/pppppppp/8/3nP3/3P4/8/PPP2PPP/RNBQKBNR b KQkq - 0 3`
- **Difficulty:** ★★☆☆☆ (2/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material is level. Central pawns: White pawn on d4, White pawn on e5. Development: White has 0 minor piece(s) out, Black 1. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. It is Black's move.

**Why this move.** 3.d4 advances a pawn to d4. The Scotch strike: open the centre immediately, before Black has finished developing. After ...exd4 White recaptures with a piece that lands actively in the centre.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** This is the Scotch. Instead of slow Italian manoeuvring, White forces matters in the centre on move three, taking the initiative while Black's pieces are still at home.

**What it prevents.** Denies Black the comfortable, slow development of the Italian/Ruy structures — the centre opens before Black's pieces are ready for it.

**Tactical checks.** No immediate forcing tactics: no checks, no winning captures and no loose pieces. The position is calm and about plans, not tricks.

**Plans after this move.**
- Recapture on d4 with the knight — it belongs in the centre.
- If knights come off on d4, retake with the queen and build Nc3/Bg5/O-O-O.
- Develop fast and keep Black reacting; the initiative is the point.

**Memory hook.** Scotch = strike with d4 before they're ready.

**Mistake warning.** Don't prepare d4 slowly with c3 here (that's a different opening) — the strike works *because* it comes before Black is developed.

---

### 2.Nf3

- **Line:** `e4 Nf6 Nf3`
- **Side to move:** Black
- **FEN:** `rnbqkb1r/pppppppp/5n2/8/4P3/5N2/PPPP1PPP/RNBQKB1R b KQkq - 2 2`
- **Difficulty:** ★☆☆☆☆ (1/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material is level. Central pawns: White pawn on e4. Development: White has 1 minor piece(s) out, Black 1. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. Loose/attacked pieces — White: pawn on e4. It is Black's move.

**Why this move.** 2.Nf3 develops/repositions the knight to f3. Develops with a threat (the e5-pawn). Every developing move that also creates a threat gains time — the opponent must react instead of developing.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** Step two of the plan: develop with a threat, provoke a committal defence of e5, and only then strike with d4.

**What it prevents.** Stops liberating ideas based on ...d5 or holding e5 for free — Black must commit a defender first.

**Candidate alternatives.**
- `e5` _(theoretical)_ — Gains space and time by kicking Black's pieces. The repertoire answers 2...Nd5. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.

**Tactical checks.** Tempting captures for Black: Nxe4. Loose White pieces: pawn on e4 (undefended).

**Plans after this move.**
- Play d4 next and open the centre while Black is committed to e5.
- Meet 2...Nf6 (Petroff) with the critical 3.Nxe5.
- Against 2...d6, exchange in the centre and aim for the queen-trade endgame.

**Memory hook.** Develop with a threat — make them defend, not develop.

**Mistake warning.** Don't drift: keep developing and castle before starting an attack, and don't push pawns in front of your own king without a reason.

---

### 1...g6

- **Line:** `e4 g6`
- **Side to move:** White
- **FEN:** `rnbqkbnr/pppppp1p/6p1/8/4P3/8/PPPP1PPP/RNBQKBNR w KQkq - 0 2`
- **Difficulty:** ★★☆☆☆ (2/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material is level. Central pawns: White pawn on e4. Development: White has 0 minor piece(s) out, Black 0. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. It is White's move.

**Why this move.** 1...g6 advances a pawn to g6. The Modern: Black fianchettoes and lets White build. The answer is principled — take the full centre and develop naturally.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** This move follows the repertoire's guiding ideas: open the centre early (d4!), develop with threats, centralise — even the queen, when she cannot be harassed — and steer for structures Black must defend precisely: queen-trade endgames, doubled pawns, kings stuck in the centre. Against g6, the repertoire continues 2.d4.

**What it prevents.** More a constructive move than a preventive one: it denies the opponent easy targets and keeps the position free of weaknesses.

**Candidate alternatives.**
- `e5` _(theoretical)_ — The classical reply: Black stakes an equal claim in the centre. The repertoire answers 2.Nf3. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `d5` _(theoretical)_ — The Scandinavian: Black challenges e4 at once, accepting an early queen excursion after the capture on d5 — time White will collect with developing moves. The repertoire answers 2.exd5. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `e6` _(theoretical)_ — The French: solid but a little passive. The repertoire answers 2.d4. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `c5` _(theoretical)_ — The Sicilian: Black fights for d4 with a wing pawn and aims for unbalanced play. The repertoire answers 2.c3. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.

**Tactical checks.** No immediate forcing tactics: no checks, no winning captures and no loose pieces. The position is calm and about plans, not tricks.

**Plans after this move.**
- Finish development quickly — every piece toward the centre.
- Keep or increase the central superiority; meet wing play with central play.
- Choose the right castling: short in quiet lines, long in the Qxd4 setup.

**Memory hook.** Remember 1...g6 as a natural building move in the Scotch Game setup.

**Mistake warning.** Don't drift: keep developing and castle before starting an attack, and don't push pawns in front of your own king without a reason.

### 1...f5

- **Side to move:** White
- **FEN:** `rnbqkbnr/ppppp1pp/8/5p2/4P3/8/PPPP1PPP/RNBQKBNR w KQkq - 0 2`
- **Difficulty:** ★★☆☆☆ (2/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material is level. Central pawns: White pawn on e4. Development: White has 0 minor piece(s) out, Black 0. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure is still healthy and symmetrical-ish. Loose/attacked pieces — White: pawn on e4; Black: pawn on f5. It is White's move.

**Why this move.** 1...f5 advances a pawn to f5. The Fred (1...f5?!): a gambit with far more surprise value than substance — it opens the very diagonal the Black king lives on.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** The repertoire carries the full refutation: accept, develop, then the forced king hunt. Worth one focused study session. Against f5, the repertoire continues 2.exf5.

**What it prevents.** It keeps the initiative — the opponent now has to worry about the pawn on e4 rather than carrying out their own plan.

**Candidate alternatives.**
- `e5` _(theoretical)_ — The classical reply: Black stakes an equal claim in the centre. The repertoire answers 2.Nf3. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `d5` _(theoretical)_ — The Scandinavian: Black challenges e4 at once, accepting an early queen excursion after the capture on d5 — time White will collect with developing moves. The repertoire answers 2.exd5. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `e6` _(theoretical)_ — The French: solid but a little passive. The repertoire answers 2.d4. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.
- `c5` _(theoretical)_ — The Sicilian: Black fights for d4 with a wing pawn and aims for unbalanced play. The repertoire answers 2.c3. Another branch the repertoire prepares for.

**Tactical checks.** Checks for White: Qh5+. Tempting captures for White: exf5. Loose White pieces: pawn on e4 (undefended). Loose Black pieces: pawn on f5 (undefended).

**Plans after this move.**
- Finish development quickly — every piece toward the centre.
- Keep or increase the central superiority; meet wing play with central play.
- Choose the right castling: short in quiet lines, long in the Qxd4 setup.

**Memory hook.** Remember 1...f5 as a natural building move in the Scotch Game setup.

**Mistake warning.** Don't drift: keep developing and castle before starting an attack, and don't push pawns in front of your own king without a reason.

### 2.exf5

- **Line:** `e4 f5 exf5`
- **Side to move:** Black
- **FEN:** `rnbqkbnr/ppppp1pp/8/5P2/8/8/PPPP1PPP/RNBQKBNR b KQkq - 0 2`
- **Difficulty:** ★★★☆☆ (3/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material favours White by 1 point(s). Neither side has a pawn on the four central squares yet. Development: White has 0 minor piece(s) out, Black 0. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure: White has doubled pawns on the f-file. It is Black's move.

**Why this move.** 2.exf5 the pawn captures the pawn on f5. 1...f5 gives up a pawn for almost nothing and fatally weakens the king. Take it — the refutation is forcing, and it starts with keeping the material.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** Same philosophy as everywhere in this repertoire: refute by development and material, not by counter-speculation.

**What it prevents.** More a constructive move than a preventive one: it denies the opponent easy targets and keeps the position free of weaknesses.

**Tactical checks.** No immediate forcing tactics: no checks, no winning captures and no loose pieces. The position is calm and about plans, not tricks.

**Plans after this move.**
- Keep the pawn and develop toward the kingside (d4, Bd3, Nf3).
- When the checks start, follow the forced sequence exactly.
- If Black deviates, just finish development — you are simply winning.

**Memory hook.** 1...f5? Take it. Then follow the checklist to the king.

**Mistake warning.** Half-remembering the Fred refutation and improvising in the middle — if unsure, keep the pawn, develop simply and castle; you don't need the most brutal line to be winning.

### 5.fxg6

- **Line:** `e4 f5 exf5 Nf6 d4 d5 Bd3 g6 fxg6`
- **Side to move:** Black
- **FEN:** `rnbqkb1r/ppp1p2p/5nP1/3p4/3P4/3B4/PPP2PPP/RNBQK1NR b KQkq - 0 5`
- **Difficulty:** ★★★★★ (5/5)
- **Explanation source:** heuristic

**Position summary.** Material favours White by 2 point(s). Central pawns: White pawn on d4, Black pawn on d5. Development: White has 1 minor piece(s) out, Black 1. Kings — White is still in the centre (can still castle); Black is still in the centre (can still castle). Pawn structure: White has doubled pawns on the g-file; Black has an isolated pawn on the h-file. It is Black's move.

**Why this move.** 5.fxg6 the pawn captures the pawn on g6. Part of a forced refutation: a check, a capture or a direct threat that drags the Black king further up the board. Move order matters — follow the sequence exactly.

**Scotch repertoire logic.** The one truly forced line in the repertoire: the Fred refutation is a sequence of checks and threats worth learning move by move — it wins on the spot.

**What it prevents.** Each check denies the king a breathing move — one slow move and Black would consolidate, so the sequence prevents exactly that.

**Tactical checks.** No immediate forcing tactics: no checks, no winning captures and no loose pieces. The position is calm and about plans, not tricks.

**Plans after this move.**
- Finish development quickly — every piece toward the centre.
- Keep or increase the central superiority; meet wing play with central play.
- Choose the right castling: short in quiet lines, long in the Qxd4 setup.

**Memory hook.** In the Fred: if it's not a check or a threat, it's not the move.

**Mistake warning.** Inserting a 'safe' developing move in the middle of the forced sequence — that's the one way to let the king escape. Learn it as one chunk.
